>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=233)** Select the root OU, click the actions button and click "Create new."
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=239)** Enter a name for the OU and click "Create."
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=246)** Now we have the OU but it is empty.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=249)** Let's add the member account to it.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=251)** Select the member account, click the actions button and then click "Move."
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=257)** Select the OU and click "Move AWS account."
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=262)** The member account is now part of the OU.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=266)** On the left navigation pane is the services section.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=271)** Here, you will find compatible services for which you can enable trusted access.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=276)** Doing so allows the service to perform operations across all accounts in your organization.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=283)** The policy section allows you to perform additional management of your organization.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=288)** For example, you can configure organization wide backup policies and tagging policies.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=295)** Service control policies or SCPs, allow you to define the maximum permissions for member accounts.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=302)** It acts as a guardrail, setting limits on the member account actions.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=306)** Let's create an SCP that will prevent member accounts from launching new EC2 instances.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=313)** First, click "Service control policies" and then enable it.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=318)** By default, AWS creates an SCP called full AWS access, which allows all operations and it is attached to the root of the organization.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=328)** This means all member accounts have full access.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=332)** For our purposes, we'll create a new SCP.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=336)** Click "Create policy."
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=339)** Enter a name for the policy.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=342)** Scrolling down, you will find the statement outline.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=347)** Here, you will need to define the actions and resources.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=351)** Note that the policy has the effect set to deny.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=354)** Remember, an SCP acts as a guardrail by limiting the maximum permissions, so it uses the deny statement.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=363)** On the right side, you will find a list of services.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=366)** From here, select the EC2 service.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=369)** Then you will need to select the actions you want to deny.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=373)** For our purpose, we're going to use the run instances action.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=378)** This is the action that allows you to launch EC2 instances.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=382)** Then we must add the required resources.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=385)** Scroll down and click "Add a resource" From the resource type dropdown, select "All Resources" then click "Add resource."
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=395)** Just to review, we have an SCP that denies the EC2 run instances action on all resources.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=402)** Scroll down and click "Create policy."
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=406)** Now it's time to apply the policy to the member account.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=410)** Back to AWS accounts, expand the OU and click the member account.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=416)** Scroll down to the policy section and click "Attach."
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=421)** Select the SCP you just created and click "Attach policy."
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=427)** Now, users of the member account, including the root user, won't be able to execute the actions defined in the policy.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=434)** Even though the full AWS access policy is attached, the deny action in the custom policy will take precedence and prevent the user from performing the actions.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=444)** Let's quickly verify this.
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=447)** I'm back over here to the member account, I'll go to the EC2 service.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=453)** To launch a new EC2 instance, I'll click "Instances" on the left navigation pane and then click the "Launch instances" button.
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=463)** I'll quickly provide a name.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=465)** I'll use the default, AMI.
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=468)** I do not need a key pair to log in, so I'll select the appropriate option, proceed without a key pair and I'll choose an existing security group, the default security group.
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=479)** Then I'll click the 'Launch instance" button.
>
> **[8:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=484)** And we are presented with an error stating that the action is unauthorized because there is an explicit deny in a service control policy.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=493)** As you can see, SCPs are simple yet powerful tools to limit actions of member accounts.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/organizing-multiple-accounts-with-aws-organizations?u=76281980&t=500)** And also, using AWS Organizations can be a strategic architectural decision, allowing you to easily organize and gain control over multiple AWS accounts.

#### Governing multiple accounts using AWS Control Tower
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=0)** AWS Control Tower is a service commonly used to manage the security and compliance of multiple accounts.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=7)** It allows you to govern a multi-account AWS environment following best practices.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=12)** These best practices are available as controls that you can select from a pre-configured list.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=18)** Think of controls as rules that help with the governance of your account.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=23)** Controls are categorized based on their behavior and guidance.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=27)** Controls are classified into three types based on their behavior.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=31)** They can be preventive, detective, and proactive.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=35)** Preventive controls disallow actions that lead to policy violations.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=41)** For example, a preventive control requires that attached EBS volumes, also known as Elastic Block Store volumes are configured to encrypt data at rest.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=51)** Encryption protects against unauthorized access to your data.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=55)** There is another preventive control that disallows bucket policy changes for S3 buckets.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=61)** Bucket policies control access to S3 buckets and their objects.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=66)** Next, we have detective controls.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=69)** Detective controls check for non-compliance of resources.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=73)** For example, a detective control detects EC2 instances that have a public IPv4 address.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=80)** Another detective control requires that RDS instances, also known as Relational Database Service instances, should have automatic backups enabled.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=90)** Next, we have proactive controls.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=93)** Proactive controls scan resources before they are provisioned to ensure they remain compliant.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=99)** Non-compliant resources are not provisioned.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=103)** For example, a proactive control requires an S3 bucket to have block public access settings configured.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=110)** Another proactive control requires that Application Load Balancers should have logging activated.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=117)** In addition to behavior, controls are also grouped based on guidance, which refers to the recommended practice for how to apply each control.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=125)** This can be mandatory, strongly recommended and elective.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=130)** Control guidance is independent of behavior.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=134)** Mandatory controls are always applied and cannot be changed.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=138)** For example, a mandatory control enables CloudTrail in all available regions.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=144)** CloudTrail is the logging service of AWS.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=147)** Another mandatory control, this allows changes to Amazon CloudWatch set up by Control Tower.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=153)** CloudWatch is the monitoring service of AWS, and Control Tower automatically configures it at the time of initial setup.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=162)** Next, is strongly recommended controls.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=164)** Strongly recommended controls enforce common best practices for well-architected multi-account environments.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=172)** For example, a strongly recommended control, this allows the creation of access keys for the root user.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=178)** Another strongly recommended control detects whether public read access to S3 buckets is allowed.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=185)** Next is elective controls.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=188)** Elective controls allow you to track or lock down actions that are commonly restricted in enterprise environments.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=195)** For example, an elective control disallows changes to logging configuration for S3 buckets.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=201)** Another elective control detects whether MFA or multi-factor authentication is enabled for IAM users.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=209)** You will find the full list of these controls in the documentation.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=213)** Strongly recommended and elective controls are optional and not enabled by default, but enabling them will improve your security posture.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=222)** With Control Tower, you can also provision new AWS accounts that adhere to your security and compliance requirements.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=230)** When you configure Control Tower, it creates a landing zone.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=235)** It is an enterprise wide container for your AWS accounts that is based on security and compliance best practices.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=242)** As part of the landing zone, AWS creates a security OU, also known as an organizational unit that contains two shared accounts; a log archive account and an audit account.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=255)** The log archive account contains logging information for all enrolled accounts.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=260)** The audit account can be used to perform audits and automated security operations like remediation.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=267)** Now let's go to the AWS Management Console and see how to configure Control Tower.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=273)** Here, I'm logged in as the root account.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=277)** This account has AWS organizations configured, but it is not a requirement to configure Control Tower.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=284)** You will find Control Tower under the "management and governance" section.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=289)** To use Control Tower, you will need to set up a landing zone.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=292)** Here, you can review its features and pricing.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=296)** Next, you will need to specify the home region.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=300)** This is where resources in shared accounts are provisioned.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=303)** Bear in mind, this cannot be changed once the landing zone has been set up.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=308)** Next, you can select the regions that you'd like to govern with Control Tower.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=313)** In production accounts, this would be a useful setting as it allows you to control which regions are allowed for resource deployment.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=320)** Scrolling down, you will find the region deny setting.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=324)** When this is set to enabled, those regions not governed by Control Tower cannot be accessed.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=330)** For now, we'll leave it at the default setting and go to the next step.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=335)** Here, you can configure the name of the foundational OU.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=339)** By default, it is set to security.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=342)** This OU will contain the log archive and audit accounts.

> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=353)** For now, we'll opt out of this and click "Next."

> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=382)** Let's go to the next step.

> **[9:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=569)** When using Control Tower, a common question is how it integrates with AWS Organizations, since both services allow you to manage multiple accounts.
>
> **[9:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=578)** If you're looking for a simple solution to consolidate multiple AWS accounts into a single organization and manage it centrally, AWS Organizations is the answer.
>
> **[9:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=589)** It also allows you to consolidate billing and use service control policies, or SCPs, to control permissions across accounts.
>
> **[9:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=598)** If you're looking for a solution that allows you to set up your AWS accounts according to security and compliance best practices, AWS Control Tower is the answer.
>
> **[10:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=607)** It creates a baseline environment with best practices and enforces governance using controls.
>
> **[10:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=614)** If you're looking for account organization, policy control, and governance, use both AWS Organizations and Control Tower.
>
> **[10:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/governing-multiple-accounts-using-aws-control-tower?u=76281980&t=621)** Service control policies and IAM roles and policies can be used for fine-grained access control, while controls available with Control Tower can be used for governance and best practice enforcement.

#### IAM Identity Center
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=0)** So we've seen that AWS Organizations and AWS Control Tower can be used to manage multiple AWS accounts.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=7)** From a solution architecture perspective, another challenge with managing accounts is centralizing user access management.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=14)** Without a centralized solution, your users must remember the credentials for each separate account.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=21)** AWS IAM Identity Center provides a solution to this problem.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=26)** It allows you to centralize and simplify access management for multiple AWS accounts, AWS applications, and SAML enabled cloud applications such as Salesforce, Microsoft 365, and more.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=40)** IAM Identity Center was previously known as AWS Single Sign-On.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=44)** With Identity Center, you can configure the following identity sources.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=49)** First, is the Identity Center directory.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=52)** The service creates this and it is your default identity source.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=57)** You can also use Active Directory as your identity source.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=60)** This can be an AWS-managed Microsoft Active Directory or your self-managed directory.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=66)** Identity Center will synchronize user, group, and membership information from the Active Directory.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=72)** You can also use an external identity provider.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=76)** It can be a SAML 2.0 supported identity provider such as Okta or Microsoft Entra ID.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=83)** If you're already using Active Directory or an external identity provider, AWS recommends connecting it to Identity Center.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=91)** Identity Center can be used with or without AWS Organizations.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=97)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and see how to configure Identity Center.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=103)** Here, I'm logged in as the root user of this AWS account.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=107)** This account has AWS Organizations configured.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=111)** Use the services dropdown menu at the top then go to security, identity and compliance and then go to the IAM Identity Center service.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=121)** Once you're here, click the "Enable" button.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=125)** Doing so creates an instance of Identity Center.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=129)** Note that by default, the source is set to Identity Center directory.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=134)** Here is the access portal URL that your users can use to access AWS accounts and cloud applications.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=142)** Under the settings section on the left navigation pane, you will find the identity source details and you have the option to change the identity source or customize the AWS access portal URL.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=154)** Under the authentication section, you can configure multi-factor authentication.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=160)** By default, it requires users to register an MFA device at sign in.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=164)** Because this is a lab environment, I'll change this behavior.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=169)** I'll set it to allow them to sign in and click "Save changes."
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=173)** For production accounts, it is recommended to use multi-factor authentication.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=179)** Under the user section, you can configure users who can use the access portal to access AWS accounts and applications.

#### VPC and subnets
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=0)** An important element of any cloud based solution is its network configuration. In AWS, this is configured as VPCs and subnets.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=9)** A VPC or a Virtual Private Cloud is a logically isolated virtual network.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=15)** A VPC resides in a single AWS region.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=19)** From an architectural perspective, a VPC resembles a traditional network in an on-premises data center.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=26)** A VPC contains subnets.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=29)** A subnet is a range of IP addresses in the VPC.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=33)** A subnet resides in a single availability zone.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=36)** Here is an example.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=38)** Here is a VPC called Production VPC.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=41)** It is in the US-East-1 region and it has an IP address range of 10.0.0.0/16.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=48)** The VPC has two subnets; subnet A and subnet B.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=53)** Subnet A is in the US East, 1a availability zone and has an IP address range of 10.0.1.0/24.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=61)** While subnet B is in the US East, 1b availability zone and has the IP address range 10.0.2.0/24.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=71)** The IP ranges of both subnets belong to the VPC IP range.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=76)** You launch resources such as EC2 instances and EBS volumes within the subnets.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=83)** The following factors should be considered when designing your VPCs and subnets: network isolation, IP addressing, security, connectivity, high availability, and compliance.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=97)** Let's talk about each of these.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=99)** Starting with network isolation.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=102)** By default, VPCs are isolated.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=105)** This means resources in different VPCs do not talk to each other, even if they are in the same AWS region.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=113)** This allows you to isolate or segregate resources based on function, environment, and other criteria leading to a secure design.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=122)** Next is IP addressing.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=124)** When defining the VPC IP address range, choose a CIDR block or IP address range that is large enough to accommodate your current resources and allows for scalability in future.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=136)** VPCs are isolated, but they can be peered only if their IP ranges don't overlap.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=142)** Peering refers to connecting two VPCs.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=145)** Subnets can be configured as public or private.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=149)** Public subnets have a route to an Internet gateway, allowing access to the Internet.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=155)** They also allow incoming connections from the Internet.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=159)** This is typically used by web servers.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=162)** Private subnets have a route to a network address translation or NAT device allowing access to the Internet.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=169)** The NAT device does not allow connection requests originating from the Internet.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=174)** This is typically used by your database servers.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=177)** Carefully planning your subnets will improve the security of your solution.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=182)** The next factor to keep in mind is security.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=185)** Security groups and network access control lists, or NACLs can be used to filter inbound and outbound traffic.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=193)** Security groups are applied to instances while NACLs are applied to subnets.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=199)** We'll talk more about these in an upcoming video.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=202)** Next is connectivity.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=204)** VPCs provide various connectivity options.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=208)** VPC peering allows direct connectivity between VPCs.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=211)** Instances in either VPC communicate with each other as if they are in the same network.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=217)** Peering VPCs may belong to the same or different AWS accounts but should not have overlapping IP address ranges.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=225)** Customers who need to connect their on-premises environment can do so using AWS VPN or Direct Connect.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=233)** AWS VPN allows you to create an IPsec site-to-site VPN connection with your on-premises network.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=240)** AWS Direct Connect establishes a dedicated private connection from your on-premises network to your VPC.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=246)** We'll cover these in detail in the next video.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=250)** Next is high availability.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=253)** Spreading resources such as your application servers in multiple subnets will allow them to be highly available and fault tolerant.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=261)** This is especially true when using services like Elastic Load Balancing and Auto Scaling.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=266)** We'll cover these in later videos.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=269)** Finally, we have compliance. Because a VPC is associated with an AWS region, it is important to consider regulatory requirements such as data residency.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=280)** For example, if you're a bank operating in the U.K.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=283)** you may be required to host your resources and data in an AWS region in the U.K.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=289)** The following are some additional architectural considerations for VPCs.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=295)** VPC support both IPv4 and IPv6 addresses.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=300)** The largest IPv4 VPC size is /16 and the smallest is /28.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=306)** For IPv6, the largest is /44 and the smallest is /60.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=312)** You can bring your public IPv4 and IPv6 addresses to your AWS account.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=318)** These can be assigned to your resources.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=321)** Subnets can be shared with other accounts in the same AWS organization.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=326)** Doing so reduces the number of VPCs you create while maintaining separate accounts for billing.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=332)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and create a new VPC and subnet and share it with another account.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=341)** Here, I'm logged in to the Management Console.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=344)** This account has AWS Organizations configured.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=347)** First, let's create a new VPC and subnet.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=351)** Use the services dropdown menu at the top.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=354)** Go to networking and content delivery and then go to the VPC service.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=360)** There is a default VPC in every region.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=363)** You will find this under your VPCs.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=367)** To create a new VPC, click "Create VPC" then provide a name and IP address range.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=375)** I'll call this as ProdVPC and I'll use the IP address range 10.0.0.0/16.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=384)** Scroll down and click "Create VPC."
>

>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=391)** Now, let's create two subnets in this VPC.

>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=423)** I'll do this one more time to create the second subnet.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=427)** Select the VPC.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=430)** Give the subnet a name.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=432)** I'll call the subnet-B.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=434)** Select an availability zone and then provide the IP address range.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=440)** For this one, I'll use 10.0.2.0/24 and click "'Create subnet."
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=447)** Now, we've created the two subnets; subnet A and subnet B.
>
> **[7:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=451)** Let's share one of these subnets with an account in the organization.
>

>
> **[8:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=481)** I've already enabled the setting for my organization.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=485)** Now you can create a resource share.
>

>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpc-and-subnets?u=76281980&t=493)** Give the share a name.

>

>

>

#### VPN and Direct Connect
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=0)** When migrating workloads from on-premises environments to the AWS cloud, a key architectural decision is connectivity.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=8)** How are your on-premises resources going to communicate with your AWS resources?
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=13)** AWS has two options to establish secure connectivity between your on-premises resources and those in the cloud.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=20)** First, is AWS site-to-site VPN and the other is Direct Connect.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=26)** A site-to-site VPN is a secure connection between your on-premises equipment and your VPCs.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=33)** To configure the VPN connection, you will need a virtual private gateway and a customer gateway device.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=40)** The virtual private gateway is the VPN concentrator or termination point on the AWS side of the VPN connection.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=48)** You create this within your AWS account and attach it to a VPC.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=52)** Alternatively, you can also use a transit gateway as the termination point.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=59)** A transit gateway is a transit hub that you can use to interconnect your VPCs and your on-premises networks.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=66)** The customer gateway device is a physical device or a software application on your side of the site-to-site VPN connection.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=75)** AWS has detailed documentation to help you configure the site-to-site VPN connection.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=81)** Each site-to-site VPN connection has two tunnels, each terminating in a different availability zone.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=87)** This is for redundancy purposes.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=90)** When one tunnel becomes unavailable, network traffic is automatically routed to the other tunnel.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=96)** While the site-to-site VPN is an easy way to establish private connectivity between your on-premises environment and AWS, the following architectural considerations should be reviewed.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=107)** First is performance.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=110)** The site-to-site VPN connection is established over the public Internet and hence its performance may not be ideal for large data transfers and latency sensitive applications.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=121)** Next is cost.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=123)** Since the connection relies on the Internet, data transfer costs depend on your Internet service provider agreements and can sometimes be high depending on your usage.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=134)** Next is security.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=136)** Site-to-site VPNs use protocols such as IPsec and are considered secure mechanisms to establish site-to-site connectivity.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=144)** If you'd like to improve the security further, consider Direct Connect, which is a dedicated connection.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=151)** Direct Connect is a dedicated or private connection between your on-premises environment and AWS.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=158)** This connection bypasses the public Internet, providing more bandwidth and lower latency.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=164)** Consider Direct Connect when you have applications that have high-performance requirements and need low latency.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=172)** It can also be considered when performing large-scale data migration from on-premises to AWS.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=178)** Pricing for Direct Connect is based on two factors.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=182)** Capacity, which is the maximum rate that data can be transferred through the connection and port hours, which is the time that a port is provisioned for your use.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=192)** Both site-to-site VPN and Direct Connect allow you to connect your on-premises environment to AWS securely.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=200)** The decision on which one to use depends on security, cost, and performance considerations and the nature of your workloads.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=209)** Site-to-site VPN is a good choice if you're looking for a cost effective solution and your applications aren't very demanding.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/vpn-and-direct-connect?u=76281980&t=217)** However, if your applications are data intensive or performance sensitive and you need a dedicated connection, Direct Connect is an excellent choice.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** vpn (12), aws (11), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (11)
> **Definitions:** is a  (6), is an  (2)
> **Code Keywords:** private (4), public (2)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)

#### Controlling inbound traffic with security groups and network ACLs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=0)** A key aspect of securing your resources in AWS is to control traffic both inbound and outbound.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=7)** AWS has two features that allow you to control traffic; network access control lists, also known as network ACLs and security groups.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=17)** First, let's talk about network ACLs.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=19)** Network ACLs allow or deny inbound and outbound traffic at the subnet level.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=26)** All VPCs have a default network ACL that allows all inbound and outbound traffic.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=32)** You can modify the default network ACL, and you can also create a custom ACL and associate it with a subnet.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=40)** You can associate a network ACL with multiple subnets.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=44)** However, a subnet can be associated with only one ACL.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=48)** With network ACLs, you can create both allow and deny rules.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=54)** Each rule has a number associated with it.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=57)** Rules are evaluated in order, starting with the lowest number rule.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=62)** If traffic matches a rule, the rule is applied and evaluation is stopped.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=67)** An important thing to bear in mind is that network ACLs are stateless.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=72)** This means they do not maintain traffic state information.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=76)** For example, here is an EC2 instance in a subnet.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=80)** A network ACL rule allows specific outbound traffic, but responses to that traffic are not automatically allowed, so traffic must be explicitly allowed in both directions.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=92)** Also, because a network ACL works at the subnet level, if inbound traffic is allowed, it can reach all instances in that subnet.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=101)** Network ACLs do not evaluate traffic routed within a subnet.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=106)** It only applies to traffic entering or leaving the subnet.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=109)** You can use security groups to control traffic at the instance level.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=114)** A security group is like a virtual firewall.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=117)** It controls traffic that is allowed to reach and leave the resources that it is associated with.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=123)** For example, a security group associated with an EC2 instance controls its inbound and outbound traffic.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=130)** Unlike a network ACL that works at the subnet level and controls traffic to all resources in the subnet, the security group works at the instance or resource level.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=141)** Think of it as a wrapper around the resource.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=144)** Security groups can be associated with EC2 instances, RDS instances, Elastic Load Balancers, Elastic Network Interfaces, and more.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=155)** A security group can only contain allow rules.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=159)** It cannot contain deny rules.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=161)** Anything you do not allow explicitly is denied.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=166)** A security group can be used by multiple resources, and multiple security groups can be associated with a resource, in which case, all security groups are aggregated to decide whether traffic should be allowed.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=179)** Security groups are stateful, meaning, they maintain traffic state information.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=184)** For example, if outbound traffic is allowed by a security group rule, the corresponding response traffic is automatically allowed.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=193)** So to summarize, here are the main differences between network ACLs and security groups.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=198)** Network ACLs work at the subnet level while security groups work at the resource level.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=204)** Network ACLs can contain both allow and deny rules while security groups contain allow rules only.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=211)** Network ACLs are stateless, while security groups are stateful.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=216)** Both these technologies are important from an architectural perspective, and combining them will allow you to find the balance between fine grained control and overall network flexibility.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=227)** With their resource level granularity, security groups make it easy to precisely control traffic for each resource.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/controlling-inbound-traffic-with-security-groups-and-network-acls?u=76281980&t=234)** Meanwhile, network ACLs operating at the subnet level allow you to manage the flow of traffic through your VPC and provide an additional layer of defense.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** acl (8), ec2 (3), aws (2), rds (1), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (2), find (1), make (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3), think of it as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1), case, (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is an  (1)

#### Securing keys and credentials
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=0)** When working in AWS, a key aspect is securely storing sensitive information such as keys and credentials.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=8)** AWS has two services that can help with this.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=11)** AWS Key Management Service, also known as KMS and AWS Secrets Manager.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=18)** AWS KMS allows you to create and store keys used for cryptographic operations such as encryption.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=25)** Encryption allows you to securely store data at rest.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=29)** With KMS, you can create and edit symmetric and asymmetric keys.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=34)** You can use these to encrypt, decrypt, and re-encrypt data.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=39)** You can control access to keys using key policies and IAM policies.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=44)** You can automatically rotate the cryptographic material of a key.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=48)** Cryptographic material refers to the string of bits used in a cryptographic algorithm.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=54)** You can also use KMS to delete keys.

> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=65)** The only exception to this is when you create an asymmetric key pair, in which case, you can export the public key outside of AWS.

> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=73)** The private key never leaves AWS unencrypted.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=77)** With KMS, keys are either AWS or customer-managed.

> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=82)** AWS-managed keys are automatically created by the service.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=86)** You can use these to encrypt objects in an S3 bucket, encrypt an EBS volume, and more.

> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=276)** Now, let's talk about AWS Secrets Manager.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=280)** As the name suggests, it is a secrets management service that helps protect secrets related to your applications and services.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=288)** It allows you to manage and retrieve database and application credentials, API keys OAuth tokens, SSH keys, and other secrets.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=298)** Hard-coding credentials in the application source code is a security risk, as anyone with access to the code can access the credentials.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=307)** Instead, you can store the credentials in Secrets Manager and configure the application to call the service whenever it needs the credentials.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=315)** Secrets Manager encrypts your secrets at rest using KMS keys.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=320)** When you retrieve a secret, Secrets Manager decrypts the secret and transmits it securely over TLS or Transport Layer Security.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=328)** You can control access to Secrets Manager using IAM policies.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=333)** This means only authorized users have access to the secrets.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=338)** You can also configure IAM cross-account access, allowing users in other accounts to access secrets in your account.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=345)** We spoke about this in an earlier video.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=348)** You can configure Secrets Manager to automatically rotate your secrets based on a schedule.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=354)** It does so using a Lambda function.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=357)** Back over here to the AWS Console, let's see how to store a secret with Secrets Manager.
