>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=406)** Next, you'll need to specify the key to use for encryption.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=411)** You can use the service default key or use any customer manage key.

> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=417)** In the next step, provide a name for the secret then apply any required tags, and you also have the option to configure resource permissions and replicate the secret to other regions.
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=431)** In the next step, you can configure automatic rotation.
>

>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=455)** You can use this in your application source code.
>
> **[7:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=459)** Scroll down and click "Store."
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=463)** The secret has been stored and can now be accessed by your applications.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=469)** As we've just seen, KMS and Secrets Manager simplify cryptography key and secret management.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-keys-and-credentials?u=76281980&t=475)** Using these tools in your solutions improves your security posture and aligns your architecture with best practices in data protection.

#### Securing data in transit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=0)** In the last video, we talked about key management service, which allows you to create keys to encrypt and secure data at rest.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=8)** Now, let's talk about securing data in transit, that is, data moving over the network.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=13)** Data in transit is secured using SSL/TLS certificates.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=18)** AWS has a service called AWS Certificate Manager or ACM that allows you to provision and manage SSL/TLS certificates.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=27)** Secure Sockets Layer, or SSL and Transport Layer Security or TLS or cryptographic protocols that secure communications on the network.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=37)** TLS is the successor of SSL, and both protocols use certificates to secure communications.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=44)** Let's understand how it works.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=46)** When you visit a website, your web browser wants to ensure it's talking to the right website and not an imposter trying to trick you.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=54)** To do this, your browser requests the server certificate.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=59)** When the server presents its certificate, the browser verifies that it is issued by a trusted entity, also known as a trusted certificate Authority, or CA.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=68)** CAs are organizations that are widely recognized and trusted to issue SSL/TLS certificates.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=76)** Your browser comes pre-installed with a list of these trusted CAs.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=80)** Once the browser verifies the server's identity using the certificate it presented, it establishes a secure channel for communication using the public key contained in the server certificate.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=92)** A warning is shown to the user if the browser cannot verify the server's identity.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=97)** Manually purchasing, storing, and renewing SSL certificates can be complex.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=104)** AWS Certificate Manager makes it easy to request a publicly trusted certificate and deploy it on supported AWS resources.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=113)** You can also use ACM to manage private SSL/TLS certificates.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=118)** Public certificates are used by resources on the public Internet, while private certificates are used on internal or private networks.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=126)** ACM also allows you to import certificates issued by third party certificate authorities.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=132)** ACM certificates are supported by many AWS services, including Elastic Load Balancers, Amazon CloudFront, AWS Elastic Beanstalk, Amazon API Gateway, and more.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=145)** You will find the full list of supported services in the documentation.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=150)** ACM certificates can secure single domain names like [example.com](https://example.com).
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=155)** Multiple domain names like [example.com](https://example.com) and [example.net](https://example.net) and wildcard domains like *.[example.com](https://example.com).
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=164)** It can also secure any number of subdomains.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=168)** ACM certificates are trusted by all major browsers, including Google Chrome, Mozilla Firefox, Microsoft Internet Explorer and Edge, and Apple Safari.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=179)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and see how to use ACM to manage certificates.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=186)** I'm here at the AWS Management Console.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=188)** You will find Certificate Manager under the "Security, Identity, and Compliance" section.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=194)** Once you're here, you can request a certificate, import a certificate obtained from a third party, or create a private certificate authority.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=203)** As the name suggests, a private CA is a certificate authority exclusive to you.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=208)** You can use it to issue private certificates to secure communications between resources in your private network.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=215)** Let's request a new certificate.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=219)** I can only request a public certificate because I do not have a private CA.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=224)** In the next step, enter the fully qualified domain name to be associated with the certificate.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=230)** Next, select how you will validate ownership.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=233)** This ensures that you own the domain for which you are requesting the certificate.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=238)** DNS validation requires adding DNS entries to the domain's DNS configuration.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=243)** Alternatively, you can choose email validation.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=247)** Next, you can choose the encryption algorithm for your certificate.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=252)** Add any required tags and then click "Request."
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=256)** After requesting it, you will see the DNS record that you must create to validate ownership.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=262)** You must do this in the Domain Registrars portal where you own the domain.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=266)** You can also import a certificate by providing the certificate body and private key.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=272)** Once the certificate is issued or imported, you can use it with supported services.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-data-in-transit?u=76281980&t=278)** As you can see, AWS Certificate Manager is easy to configure and simplifies the management of SSL/TLS certificates.

#### Security best practices
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's wrap up this section by talking about some security best practices.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=4)** Number 1: Avoid using the root account for everyday activities.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=9)** Instead, create IAM user accounts and use them for routine tasks.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=14)** Use the root account to perform tasks that really require it.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=18)** For example, to change your account settings.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=21)** Enable multi-factor authentication for the root user and for IAM users.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=27)** Implement least privilege access.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=30)** Grant only the minimum access required to perform the necessary tasks and functions.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=36)** Also, regularly review user permissions to ensure they align with their job responsibilities and remove unnecessary permissions.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=45)** Use a centralized identity provider to manage user identities.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=49)** Doing so allows you to create, manage, and revoke access from a single location.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=55)** You can use IAM Identity Center or connect to an external identity provider like Microsoft Active Directory.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=63)** Use account level separation for isolating production accounts from development and test accounts.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=69)** Doing so creates a strong logical boundary between workloads that process data of different sensitivity levels.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=76)** Use AWS Organizations to manage multiple accounts and enforce policy-based management.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=83)** Also, consider using AWS Control Tower to set up accounts based on best practices.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=89)** Configure a strong password policy to mitigate the risk of brute force attacks.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=95)** Also, rotate credentials regularly.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=99)** Use IAM roles to grant temporary access instead of using access keys.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=104)** Regularly audit credentials to identify and remove unused users, roles, permissions, policies, or credentials.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=114)** AWS has two tools that you can use for this purpose; Access Analyzer and Credential Report.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=121)** Access Analyzer identifies resources such as S3 buckets or IAM roles that are shared with external entities.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=129)** It also monitors and highlights unused roles, access keys, and passwords.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=135)** You can also use Access Analyzer to validate your IAM and policies against AWS best practices.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=142)** Here are some findings generated by Access Analyzer in my account.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=147)** As you can see, it has identified an unused role, password, and permissions.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=153)** A credential report lists all users in your account and the status of their credentials, including passwords, access keys, and multifactor authentication devices.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=164)** Both Access Analyzer and Credential report are available in the IAM service.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=170)** Next, store secrets securely. Use Secrets Manager to securely store secrets such as credentials and keys.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=178)** Create network level isolation.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=182)** Use VPCs and subnets to group resources with common reachability requirements.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=187)** For example, you should place web servers in public subnets and databases in private subnets.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=193)** Classified data.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=196)** Data can have varying sensitivity levels, and classifying it allows you to apply the appropriate security controls.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=203)** Protect data at rest and in motion.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=206)** Use encryption to protect data at rest and SSL/TLS certificates to protect data in motion.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=212)** Configure logging.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=215)** These can be useful for monitoring and incident response.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=219)** Perform vulnerability management, scan and patch your applications and infrastructure for vulnerabilities.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=226)** Finally, keep updated with security recommendations.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/security-best-practices?u=76281980&t=230)** You can do so by subscribing to AWS blogs and documentation, and to industry news such as CVE or the common vulnerabilities and exposures list.

### 2. Architecting for Resilience and High Availability

> [[#Table of Contents|↑ Back to Table of Contents]]

#### Design for failure
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's shift gears and understand how to architect resilient and highly available solutions.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=6)** As architects, it is crucial to anticipate and manage failures proactively rather than reacting to them when they occur.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=13)** So when designing your solutions, it is important to design for failure.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=18)** Before understanding how to design for failure, let's discuss two key concepts; RTO and RPO.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=25)** RTO stands for Recovery Time Objective.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=29)** It is the maximum acceptable delay between the interruption and restoration of service.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=35)** In other words, how much downtime is acceptable?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=39)** RPO stands for Recovery Point Objective.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=42)** It is the maximum acceptable amount of time since the last data recovery point.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=48)** In other words, how much data loss is acceptable?
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=51)** For example, if your RPO is one hour, you can afford to lose data generated within the last hour.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=58)** You should determine your RTO and RPO before designing for failure.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=63)** Your RTO will determine what services you use to improve your applications availability and fault tolerance.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=71)** For example, you can use services like Auto Scaling and Elastic Load Balancing to reduce your RTO.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=77)** Your RPO will determine how frequently you backup data.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=82)** Frequent automated backups are a common strategy used to reduce RPO.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=87)** Now, let's talk about design strategies that will help you create highly available and resilient solutions.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=94)** First is to design distributed systems.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=98)** AWS has its services available in many regions, and each region has multiple availability zones.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=104)** When deploying your workloads and instances, spread them across multiple availability zones and regions.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=111)** Doing so will allow you to minimize the impact of a failure in a single location.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=117)** Next, build loosely coupled architectures when designing solutions, break down large monolithic components into smaller, independent components that are loosely coupled.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=129)** This approach contains failure to only the affected components and allows them to scale independently.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=136)** Imagine a monolithic e-commerce application that sells digital products.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=141)** You can decouple this application into smaller components in the following way.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=146)** The application front end consisting of static files can be hosted on Amazon S3, which is an object storage service.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=153)** Product information can be stored on Amazon RDS or Relational Database Service.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=160)** AWS Lambda, which is a serverless compute service, can be used to process orders.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=166)** Amazon SQS or Simple Queue Service can be used as a messaging service between the components.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=173)** Amazon SNS or Simple Notification Service can send order notifications to customers.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=180)** An architecture like this minimizes the impact of a failure by containing it to the failed service and allows each service to scale independently.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=190)** The next strategy is to implement load balancing.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=193)** Load balancing distributes incoming traffic across multiple resources in multiple availability zones.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=200)** Doing so improves your applications fault tolerance, availability, and performance.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=206)** Imagine you have a global e-commerce application.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=210)** By implementing load balancing, you can direct your users to the nearest region, reducing latency and improving customer experience.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=218)** Also, a failure in one region does not affect users being served by other regions.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=224)** Next, back up your data.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=226)** In any system, it is expected that failures will occur.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=230)** Creating backup copies of important data will protect against loss and allow you to restore access quickly.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=237)** Important services like databases, have their own backup configurations that you can use to automate backups.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=244)** Alternatively, you can use AWS backup to centralize backup across AWS services.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=252)** Next, monitor for failure.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=254)** Monitoring is key to detecting and responding to failures.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=259)** Amazon CloudWatch is a monitoring service that can be used to detect failure conditions.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=265)** You can combine this with other services such as Amazon EventBridge and AWS Lambda to respond automatically when failures occur.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=273)** We'll talk about these in the next section of this course.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=277)** And finally, test reliability.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=280)** Testing is the only way to verify that your systems operate as designed and deliver the resiliency you expect.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=287)** Perform regular load testing to ensure your system can handle expected and unexpected traffic levels.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=295)** Test for high availability by simulating failure conditions and test your backup and restore processes to ensure you can recover your data and systems in case of a failure.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/design-for-failure?u=76281980&t=306)** With these strategies in mind, let's discuss the tools you can use to design for failure.

#### Elastic Load Balancing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=0)** Elastic Load Balancing is a key component when designing for failure.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=4)** It allows you to distribute incoming traffic across multiple targets such as EC2 instances and containers.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=12)** So if one instance fails, the others can continue to serve traffic.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=16)** Targets of the load balancer can be in one or more availability zones.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=22)** By spreading your instances, you can reduce the impact of an availability zone failure.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=27)** There are four load balancer types; application network, gateway, and classic.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=35)** An Application Load Balancer is recommended for distributing HTTP and HTTPS requests.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=41)** The Network Load Balancer is recommended for load balancing network or transport protocols such as TCP and UDP.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=48)** It is also recommended for applications that need high performance and low latency.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=55)** The Gateway Load Balancer is recommended when you need to load balance between third party virtual appliances available in the AWS marketplace.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=63)** For example, if you want to use a third party appliance for network traffic inspection, a Gateway Load Balancer will allow you to do this.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=73)** The Classic Load Balancer is recommended for applications built with the EC2 classic network, which does not support VPCs, though you can still use it in a VPC-based network.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=85)** Here are some key features of load balancers that make them a good choice for architecting fault tolerant applications.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=92)** Load balancers can perform health checks.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=95)** They monitor the health of registered targets and replace unhealthy ones.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=100)** By using Global Accelerator with load balancers, you can distribute incoming traffic to multiple regions.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=107)** Global Accelerator uses the Amazon Global Network to route traffic, improving the performance of your application.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=113)** And because it supports multiple regions, it improves your applications availability and fault tolerance.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=120)** Elastic Load Balancing integrates with Auto Scaling.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=124)** Auto Scaling is a service that automatically scales your application based on demand.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=129)** Because scaling is automatic, it improves the availability of your application.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=134)** It also has cost advantages because only the required capacity is provisioned.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=140)** We'll talk more about Auto Scaling in the next section of the course.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=144)** Integration with CloudWatch allows you to monitor key metrics of the load balancer, such as the active connection count, HTTP 4XX and 5XX_Count, and the RejectedConnection count.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=157)** You can also configure automated actions to occur when your metrics reach desired threshold values.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=164)** So as we've just seen, Elastic Load Balancing is a foundational element for optimizing your applications traffic distribution, availability, and fault tolerance.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/elastic-load-balancing?u=76281980&t=173)** If you'd like to learn more about Elastic Load Balancers such as their configuration, I recommend checking the course titled, AWS Elastic Load Balancing, where I discuss all four Elastic Load Balancer types in detail.

#### Architecting loosely coupled applications with SNS and SQS
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=0)** A key architectural design that makes applications fault-tolerant is loose coupling.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=5)** Coupling refers to the interdependence between different components in a system.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=10)** Loose coupling aims to minimize the dependencies between components.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=14)** This means changes to one component are less likely to have a cascading effect on other components.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=20)** Let's look at an example.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=22)** Imagine we have a monolithic web application.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=26)** The different components of the application, such as the front-end or the web tier, the application tier and the database tier are all hosted on a single server.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=36)** In this case, if the server fails, the entire application fails.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=40)** This is an example of a tightly coupled application. To reduce coupling or interdependence, we can separate the tiers.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=49)** We can host the web application and database tiers on different servers.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=54)** While this is an improvement from the previous design, it is still not highly available.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=59)** If any of the tiers fails, the web application fails.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=63)** We can solve this problem by introducing Elastic Load Balancers and Auto Scaling in this design.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=69)** We can add an Elastic Load Balancer in front of the web and application tiers.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=75)** We can also configure the servers to be part of an Auto Scaling group.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=79)** The Elastic Load Balancers will distribute incoming traffic to all servers in their tiers.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=85)** Auto Scaling will monitor the load and add or remove servers as required.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=91)** It will also automatically replace any unhealthy servers.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=95)** For the database tier, you could use any of the managed database services provided by AWS such as Amazon RDS, Amazon Aurora, and others.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=105)** With managed services, AWS takes care of scaling and high availability.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=111)** Now the application is much more fault-tolerant.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=114)** We've decoupled the components, creating a loosely coupled architecture.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=119)** Now, let's look at another example, this time focusing only on the application tier.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=125)** An e-commerce application is responsible for verifying payments, creating the order, sending a digital product, generating and sending the invoice, and then sending the order completion notification.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=138)** The application currently handles all of this.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=141)** Let's see how we can decouple it.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=143)** We can break down the larger application into smaller applications, each performing a specific task.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=149)** Let's number these applications from 1 to 5.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=153)** So the first application receives the order and then notifies the other applications to perform the remaining tasks.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=160)** In this design, the application does everything sequentially, which means if one component fails, it affects others.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=168)** To avoid this, you can use Amazon SQS or Simple Queue Service, which is a queuing or messaging service.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=176)** A queue acts as a message buffer between the sender and the receiver.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=181)** Let's insert a queue between each of the applications.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=185)** When Application 1 verifies that the payment is successful, it writes a message with payment details to Q1.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=192)** Application 2 poles Q1 for messages.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=197)** If it finds a message, it creates an order and writes the order details to Q2.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=201)** Application 3 polls Q2.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=205)** If it finds a message, it sends the digital product to the customer and writes a message with the order number to Q3.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=212)** Application 4 polls Q3.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=216)** If it finds a message, it sends an invoice with the order number and writes a message to Q4.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=221)** Application 5 polls Q4.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=225)** If it finds a message in the queue, it sends a final notification to the customer that the order has been completed.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=232)** This design allows every application to fail individually without affecting the overall flow.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=238)** Because even if an application fails, the messages will still be retained in the queue and can be consumed when the application comes back online.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=247)** While this design is great, there is still room for improvement.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=252)** SQS works on a polling mechanism.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=254)** This means every application must periodically poll the queue it is subscribed to.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=260)** So the applications will be pulling even when no orders are received.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=265)** We can address this by using Amazon SNS or Simple Notification Service.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=271)** As the name suggests, it is a notification service.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=274)** It uses a publish subscribe model, allowing applications to publish notifications to a topic which is then delivered to subscribers.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=283)** Notifications are delivered using a push mechanism, eliminating the need to pull for updates periodically.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=290)** Let's look at the updated design.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=293)** When App 1 verifies that payment is successful, it writes a message with payment details to Q1 then it sends a notification to an SNS topic called Topic 1.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=305)** App 2 is subscribed to Topic 1 so it receives the notification.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=309)** This causes it to poll Q1 for the message.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=313)** With the details found in the message, App 2 creates an order.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=317)** With this updated design, App 2 does not pull Q1 periodically.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=322)** Instead, it only pulls when a notification is received from the subscribe topic.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=327)** Now the design gets interesting.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=330)** Instead of writing to a queue, App 2 sends a notification with order details to Topic 2, which then publishes it to multiple queues, Queues 2, 3, and 4.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=341)** This design strategy is called fan out, where the same message is delivered to multiple queues.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=348)** Apps 3, 4, and 5 are also subscribed to Topic 2.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=352)** So when they receive a notification, they pull their respective queues to retrieve the message and complete their processing.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=359)** As you can see, the application is now highly decoupled.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=363)** Indeed, adding more components has cost implications, but this design allows each component to fail individually without affecting others.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=372)** If the need arises, we can debug or scale each application individually, resulting in a highly available design.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=380)** SNS and SQS are commonly used for decoupling, but there are more.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=385)** AWS has other services such as EventBridge and Step Functions, which can also be used to decouple and create event-based architectures.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/architecting-loosely-coupled-applications-with-sns-and-sqs?u=76281980&t=394)** We'll explore these in the next section of the course.

#### Host your apps with Elastic Beanstalk
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=0)** When hosting applications in the cloud, a key decision architects must make is how much control is required.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=7)** One approach is to manage both the infrastructure and the application, while another is to use a managed service that handles the infrastructure so developers can focus only on the application code.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=19)** AWS Elastic Beanstalk is a service that allows you to do this.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=23)** With Elastic Beanstalk, your developers upload their application code and the service creates the environment and runs the application.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=31)** It handles deployment details like capacity provisioning, load balancing, auto scaling, and health monitoring.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=39)** Elastic Beanstalk supports applications written in .NET, PHP, Python, Ruby, Go, Java and Node.js.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=50)** With Elastic Beanstalk, you can create either a web server or a worker environment.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=56)** The environment will determine the type of application it runs and the resources that Elastic Beanstalk provisions.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=64)** The web server environment is designed to run a website, web application, or web API that serves HTTP requests.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=72)** The worker environment is designed to run a worker application that processes workloads on demand or performs tasks on a schedule.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=81)** You can use this to offload operations or workflows from your application that take a long time to complete.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=88)** For example, your application may be processing videos or generating log archives.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=93)** Tasks like these can take a long time, and decoupling these from the application front end will allow it to stay responsive even under load.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=102)** You can configure a worker environment to handle these types of workloads.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=107)** When you create a web server environment, Elastic Beanstalk creates an Elastic Load Balancer, an Auto Scaling group, and one or more EC2 instances.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=117)** The instances host your application.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=120)** It also generates a URL for the application.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=124)** When you create a worker environment, Elastic Beanstalk creates an Auto Scaling group, one or more EC2 instances, and an SQS queue.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=134)** Your application running in the web server environment can send messages to the queue.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=139)** Demons running on the EC2 instances of the worker environment read messages from the queue and process them.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=146)** While Elastic Beanstalk is designed to simplify and abstract infrastructure details supporting your application, it does allow you to configure the details.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=155)** For example, you can select the operating system that matches your application.
>

>

>

> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=401)** Enabling the database will allow you to configure it.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=405)** So you can define the database engine, storage options, credentials, and more.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=411)** For now, I'll disable the database.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=415)** Scroll down to add any required tags and then click "Next."
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=420)** Here, you can configure the EC2 instances that will run your environment. For the root volume type, I'll select General Purpose and set the size to 10 GB.
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=431)** The default monitoring interval provided by CloudWatch is five minutes.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=435)** You can reduce it to one minute.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=438)** Next, we have the instance metadata service configuration.
>
> **[7:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=442)** Instance metadata service allows you to query metadata about the instance such as the region, IP address, AMI ID, and more.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=452)** AWS recommends using Version 2 of the service, which is more secure, so Version 1 is deactivated by default.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=460)** Then select the security groups to associate with the instance.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=464)** Next, you have capacity configuration.
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=467)** Here, you can configure the environment type.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=469)** You can configure it to be a single instance or load balanced.
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=473)** Because we are only testing, a single instance environment will work.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=477)** Fleet composition determines whether your instance fleet will contain only on-demand instances or a combination of on-demand and spot instances.
>
> **[8:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=487)** Moving on, there are other settings related to your EC2 instances and Auto Scaling group.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=493)** For now, we'll leave this at the default.
>
> **[8:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=496)** Scroll down and click "Next."
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=500)** In this step, we have settings related to updates, monitoring, and logging.
>
> **[8:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=505)** We'll keep it simple.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=506)** We'll set the health reporting to basic.
>
> **[8:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=509)** Scrolling down, we'll disable managed updates, leave other settings at the default, and click "Next."
>
> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=518)** In the last step, review your selections and then click "Submit" to create your environment.
>
> **[8:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=527)** Now, Elastic Beanstalk will create the environment and deploy the sample application.
>
> **[8:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=532)** This can take a few minutes.
>
> **[8:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=535)** After a few minutes, the deployment has completed and the health status is green.
>
> **[9:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=541)** Let's access the application by clicking on the URL.
>
> **[9:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=545)** And here is the sample application that Elastic Beanstalk has deployed.
>
> **[9:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=550)** Back over here, when your developers want to deploy another version of the application, they can simply upload the code and Elastic Beanstalk will configure the environment as required to support the application.
>
> **[9:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=563)** As you can see, Elastic Beanstalk allows your developers to focus on application development rather than infrastructure management.
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=570)** But you're not stuck with defaults or a one-size-fits-all configuration.
>
> **[9:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/host-your-apps-with-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=575)** You can configure the environment as you see fit for your application, giving you the best of both worlds; ease of automatic environment configuration and the ability to influence the configuration.

#### AWS Fargate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's shift gears a bit and talk about architecting for resilience and high availability for containerized workloads in the cloud.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=7)** Containers are a popular choice for hosting applications and workloads.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=11)** This is because containers allow you to create a packaged environment for your applications, containing the code, libraries, and other settings.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=19)** This means your containerized application will run the same way on any infrastructure that supports containers.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=25)** One of the challenges when using containers is managing the underlying infrastructure, that is, the servers that will run the containers.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=32)** AWS has a service called ECS or Elastic Container Service that allows you to run containers in the cloud.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=39)** In simple language, it is a container management service.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=42)** Being a managed service, it automatically maintains application availability by scaling your containers up or down to meet capacity requirements.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=51)** When architecting your application for ECS, you must select the launch type, that is the underlying infrastructure supporting your containers in the cloud.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=60)** You have two choices: You can use Amazon EC2 instances or AWS Fargate.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=66)** When you choose EC2 as the launch type, Elastic Container Service will launch EC2 instances to run containers on them.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=74)** You define the configuration of the EC2 instances, such as the purchasing option, which can be on-demand or spot instances, the operating system which can be Windows or Linux, the instance type, also known as the instance size and the auto scaling configuration.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=91)** Auto Scaling is used to automatically add or remove EC2 instances based on the capacity requirement.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=98)** So using the EC2 launch type gives you control over the underlying infrastructure.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=103)** You can configure the EC2 instances that host your containers. As a managed service, ECS will manage the EC2 infrastructure by monitoring its health and scaling as required.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=114)** But using ECS with Fargate gives you a simplified container management experience.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=119)** Fargate is serverless, so you don't need to choose server types or decide when to scale them.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=125)** Fargate automatically scales the infrastructure, allowing you to build applications that are much more resilient and available.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=132)** Because Fargate is serverless, the underlying infrastructure is abstracted.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=136)** This allows you to focus on building containerized applications rather than managing servers.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=141)** When you run a task in Fargate, which is a collection of containers that make up your application, Fargate does not share the underlying kernel, CPU, and memory resources or the Elastic Network Interface with another task.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=155)** This means each task runs in its own isolated boundary.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=159)** So when it comes to selecting the launch type, EC2 or Fargate, it mainly comes down to control, management overhead, and pricing.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=169)** ECS with EC2 gives more control over the underlying infrastructure also allowing you to customize it.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=175)** But ECS with Fargate gives you a serverless experience, allowing you to get started quickly without worrying about the details.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=183)** ESS with EC2 requires more management in terms of the configuration of the infrastructure, such as the instance type.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=190)** Meanwhile, ECS with Fargate requires almost no management, allowing you to focus on your containerized application.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=198)** Pricing for ECS with EC2 is based on resources such as EC2 instances and EBS volumes used to store and run your application.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/aws-fargate?u=76281980&t=208)** Pricing for ECS with Fargate is based on the amount of vCPU and memory resources your containers request.

> **Definitions:** is a  (2), known as (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (3)
> **Code Identifiers:** vcpu (1)
