#### Use cloud-native storage
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=0)** A simple yet significant step in improving the availability of your applications is switching to cloud-native storage.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=7)** Cloud-native storage refers to storage offered by the cloud provider.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=11)** Data stored in cloud storage is highly available and durable.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=15)** AWS has several storage services, including Amazon S3, Amazon Elastic Block Store, Amazon Elastic File System, Amazon FSX, and AWS Storage Gateway.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=27)** Amazon S3 or Simple Storage Service, is an object storage service.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=32)** You can use it to store any amount of data for use cases like web applications, data lakes, backup and restore, archive, and more.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=42)** S3 has storage classes for different use cases, like storing frequently or infrequently accessed data, data with unknown access patterns, and data archival.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=53)** S3 has many features that make it an excellent choice for enterprises like lifecycle management.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=59)** You can configure lifecycle policies to transition objects to other storage classes, or expire them at the end of their lifetimes.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=67)** You can configure replication to replicate objects for backup, compliance, and other use cases.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=73)** You can configure object lock to prevent objects from being deleted or overwritten for a fixed amount of time or indefinitely.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=81)** You can also set up versioning to keep multiple variants of an object to recover from unintended actions or application failures.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=89)** You can set up logging to get records for requests made to objects.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=94)** You can configure access control lists and policies to control read and write permissions for objects.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=101)** You can also configure event notifications to trigger workflows when a change is made to your S3 resources.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=108)** Most S3 storage classes are designed for at least 99.9% availability, making it a preferred choice for object storage.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=116)** Amazon EBS or Elastic Block Store provides block level storage volumes for EC2 instances.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=123)** You can mount these volumes on your instances.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=126)** EBS volumes are available in the following types.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=128)** General Purpose SSD, which provides a balance of price and performance.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=134)** It is ideal for use as boot volumes, single instance databases, and dev and test environments.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=141)** The next option is Provisioned IOPS SSD, which is designed for I/O intensive workloads like mission critical databases.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=149)** And then you have Throughput Optimized and Cold HDD volumes.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=153)** These are magnetic storage volumes for workloads that need more throughput than IOPS, for example, sequential workloads like data warehouses and log processing.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=163)** Amazon Elastic File System, or EFS, is a serverless file storage system that you can access through a file system interface.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=172)** You can use it with EC2 instances, container services, and Lambda functions.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=177)** The file system can be concurrently accessed by thousands of EC2 instances.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=182)** EFS supports Network File System or NFS Version 2, which is an industry standard protocol for accessing file systems.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=191)** NFS is commonly used in Unix and Linux environments.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=195)** For use with Windows environments, AWS has Amazon FSX.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=200)** It supports Server Message Block or SMB, which is a Windows-based protocol.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=205)** Amazon FSX also supports high performance file systems such as Lustre, NetApp ONTAP, and OpenZFS.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=213)** In addition to these storage options, AWS also has hybrid cloud storage known as Storage Gateway for use with your on-premises systems.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=222)** Your on-premises systems can use this storage as if it were locally hosted.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=228)** As you can imagine, this can be very useful in your cloud migration journey, especially if you're not ready to fully migrate your applications to the cloud.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=236)** The applications can continue to be on-premises while taking advantage of cloud-native storage.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=242)** Storage Gateway supports standard protocols such as iSCSI, SMB, and NFS, so you don't need to rewrite your applications.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=251)** Storage Gateway provides three storage interfaces; file, volume, and tape.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=258)** The file interface has two options; S3 File Gateway and FSX File Gateway.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=264)** Amazon S3 file Gateway allows your on-premises systems to store and retrieve objects in Amazon S3.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=271)** Amazon FSX File Gateway allows you to store and retrieve files in Amazon FSX for Windows File Server.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=279)** Volume Gateway provides block storage or storage volumes to your on-premises applications.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=285)** Data on the volumes is stored in Amazon S3.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=288)** Tape Gateway allows your on-premises backup application to store virtual tapes in Amazon S3.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=294)** You can archive this data to archival storage classes available in S3, known as S3 Glacier and Glacier Deep Archive.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=303)** While cloud storage has clear performance benefits, you should also consider cost.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=309)** An advantage of cloud solutions is that there is no upfront cost.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=312)** You pay as you go.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/use-cloud-native-storage?u=76281980&t=313)** However, it is important to consider the volume of data you intend to host and your future growth when computing costs.

#### Securing databases against failures
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=0)** Databases are key to almost every solution.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=3)** AWS has many database offerings, but the most important and commonly used ones include Amazon RDS or Relational Database Service, Amazon Aurora, which is also a relational database and available as part of RDS, and Amazon DynamoDB, which is a non-relational database.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=22)** To ensure your applications are resilient and highly available, it is essential to secure databases against failures.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=29)** Let's explore the features that can help you do this, starting with read replicas.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=34)** This is a feature available with Amazon RDS.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=38)** A read replica is a read-only copy of a database instance.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=42)** You can reduce the load on your primary instance by routing read request to the read replica.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=48)** Also, in case of failure, you can promote the read replica to become the primary instance.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=54)** This feature can be useful for read-heavy applications, such as news portals that host articles and blog posts and e-commerce applications where users access product details.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=65)** Amazon Aurora has a similar feature known as Aurora.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=69)** replicas.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=70)** The replicas are read-only instances which you can use to offload read queries.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=75)** Also, if the primary instance becomes unavailable, Aurora automatically promotes the reader instance, that is the replica to take its place, restoring the availability of your database.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=87)** The next feature is known as Multi-AZ.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=90)** Amazon RDS allows you to create a Multi-AZ or a Multi Availability Zone deployment.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=97)** Multi-AZ deployments have 1 or 2 standby database deployments.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=102)** The standby deployments are intended to provide failover support. If the primary deployment fails, AWS will automatically switch to the standby deployment.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=112)** The standby deployment is hosted in a different availability zone, hence the name Multi-AZ.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=118)** So the database can withstand a failure of an availability zone.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=122)** While Amazon Aurora does not have a multi-AZ deployment, it is fault-tolerant by design.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=128)** The database cluster volume spans multiple availability zones in a single AWS region, and each availability zone contains a copy of the cluster volume data.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=138)** This means that your Aurora database cluster can tolerate an availability zone's failure without any data loss.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=145)** If the primary instance in a database cluster fails, Aurora automatically promotes an existing Aurora replica if it exists to the new primary instance.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=155)** If the database cluster doesn't contain any Aurora replicas, then the primary instance is recreated.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=162)** Promoting an Aurora replica is quicker, so AWS recommends creating at least one or more replicas in two or more availability zones.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=171)** Amazon RDS and Amazon Aurora support blue/green deployments.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=175)** The blue environment is the current production environment, and the green environment is the staging environment.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=182)** A blue/green deployment keeps the production environment in sync with the staging environment.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=187)** This allows you to make changes to the database in the staging environment without affecting the production environment.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=193)** For example, you can upgrade the DB engine version, change database parameters, or make schema changes in the staging environment.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=201)** When you're ready, you can promote the staging environment to be the new production database environment so you can make changes without affecting the availability of your database.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=211)** As you can imagine, this feature can be very useful for mission critical databases.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=217)** RDS Proxy is another feature that makes RDS databases resilient.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=222)** Databases can experience issues when there are unpredictable surges in traffic, when new connections are opened at a fast rate, or when connections are oversubscribed.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=233)** RDS proxy solves this problem by establishing a database connection pool and reusing connections.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=240)** This approach prevents new connections from being opened each time.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=244)** RDS proxy also queues or throttles connections that cannot be served immediately from the pool.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=250)** While this means that our chances for latency, it prevents the database from abruptly failing.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=255)** Amazon Aurora also supports RDS proxy.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=259)** With Amazon Aurora, you can set up global databases.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=263)** A global database has one primary AWS region where your data is written and up to five read-only secondary AWS regions.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=272)** Only the primary cluster performs right operations, which are automatically replicated to the secondary regions.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=278)** This approach allows low-latency global reads and fast recovery if an outage affects an entire AWS region.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=286)** Aurora Global Databases are a good fit for applications with a worldwide footprint.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=291)** Aurora has a feature called serverless that automatically scales your database as capacity based on demand.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=298)** Without serverless, you would deploy a provision cluster.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=302)** You choose a DB instance class that has a predefined amount of memory, CPU, and I/O bandwidth.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=309)** When your workload or demand changes, you manually modify the instance class.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=315)** This works well for workloads with steady demand or a predictable surge, so you can change the configuration before the surge.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=323)** With Aurora serverless, you can create a cluster without being locked into a specific database capacity.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=330)** You specify the minimum and maximum capacity range, and Aurora scales the cluster within that range.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=336)** When working with Amazon DynamoDB, you can create global tables.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=341)** It is a multi-region, multi active database option.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=345)** Each table supports read and write operations.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=349)** This means that your database can continue to operate even if there is a degradation or failure of an entire AWS region.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=357)** This option is a great fit for large-scale global applications.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=361)** In addition to the features we've just seen, backups can be used to restore availability in case of a failure.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=368)** Amazon RDS, Aurora, and DynamoDB, allow you to create backups using AWS backup.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=375)** It is a service that allows you to centralize backups across AWS services.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/securing-databases-against-failures?u=76281980&t=380)** Let's cover this in the next video.

#### Backing up
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=0)** While it is important to architect your systems for high availability, failures can occur, and backups allow you to recover from failures.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=9)** Many services in AWS, including databases, have their own backup configuration.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=14)** Alternatively, you can use AWS backup.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=18)** AWS backup is a managed service that allows you to centralize and automate backups across AWS services.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=25)** It allows you to consolidate backup tasks that you previously performed service-by-service.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=31)** An important thing to remember is that AWS backups doesn't govern backups you take in your AWS environment outside of AWS backup.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=40)** In other words, backups you take outside of the AWS Backup Service Console cannot be managed from the service.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=47)** Important AWS services that you can backup with AWS backup include Amazon EC2, Amazon S3, Amazon Elastic Block Store, or EBS, Amazon Relational Database Service or RDS, Amazon DynamoDB, and more.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=65)** You will find the full list in the documentation.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=70)** To use AWS backup, you begin by creating a backup plan.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=74)** Within the backup plan, you define the backup schedule, retention period, and other settings.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=80)** You then apply the backup plan to your resources by tagging them then AWS backup automatically backs up your resources according to the plan.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=90)** Periodic backups are stored incrementally.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=94)** The first backup of a resource is a full copy.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=97)** For each successive incremental backup, only the changes to the resource are backed up.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=103)** AWS backup stores backups in a vault.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=107)** It is a container that stores and organizes your backups.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=111)** All accounts have a default vault and you can create additional vaults.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=116)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and see how to configure AWS backup.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=122)** I'm here at the AWS Console.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=125)** You will find the AWS Backup Service under the storage section.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=130)** Let's begin by reviewing the settings.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=134)** Here, you will find services that have been enabled for backup.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=138)** You can change this by clicking "Configure resources."
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=142)** Go to backup vaults to see your default vault.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=146)** It is encrypted with a KMS created encryption key.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=150)** You can create a vault lock to prevent manual deletion of backups and changes to the backup lifecycle.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=157)** This option can be useful for compliance purposes.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=161)** Let's create a backup plan.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=163)** Go to Backup plans on the left navigation pane and then click "Create backup plan."
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=169)** You can use a template, build a plan from scratch, or define a plan in JSON format.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=176)** Let's build a new plan.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=178)** Start by naming your plan.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=180)** Next, you'll need to define a backup rule.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=184)** A rule contains scheduling and lifecycle information.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=189)** A plan can have multiple rules.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=191)** For example, a backup plan may have two rules, each with different schedules.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=196)** Give your rule a name.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=198)** The default backup vault is chosen.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=202)** Next, define the backup frequency.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=205)** You can use a preconfigured option or define a cron expression, which is a string representation of a schedule.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=213)** I'll set the frequency to daily.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=215)** Then define your backup window.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=218)** Give it a start time, a window within which backup should start, and the time by which the backups must complete.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=226)** Next, you can enable point in time recovery.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=229)** This allows you to take continuous backups with the ability to restore within one second position.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=235)** This option is only available for a few resource types.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=239)** Next is the lifecycle configuration.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=242)** This allows you to move your backups from warm to cold storage.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=246)** Doing so reduces the storage cost.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=249)** This option is also only available for a few resource types.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=254)** Next, we have the retention period.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=256)** It defines how long your backups will be stored.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=260)** Because this is a demo environment, I'll set this to one day.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=264)** Moving on, you have the option to copy your backups to another region then you have some settings for tagging recovery points and for Windows Volume Shadow Copy service.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=276)** We'll leave this at the default and create our plan.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=280)** We've created the plan.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=283)** Now, we must assign it to resources.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=285)** Give the assignment a name.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=288)** Next, you'll need to assign a role to give AWS backup the necessary permissions to perform backups.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=295)** You can choose an existing role or let the service create one for you.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=300)** Next is resource selection.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=303)** You can include all or specific resource types.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=307)** For this assignment, I'll select specific resource types and set the type to EBS or Elastic Block Store.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=315)** By default, it applies to all volumes, but you can restrict to specific volume IDs.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=320)** Note that you can exclude specific resource IDs or refine your selection by including specific tags then click "Assign resources." Because I've assigned it to all volumes, it warns me that my current selection could back up many resources resulting in significant charges.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=340)** I'll click "Continue."
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=342)** The plan has been created.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=344)** Here, we can see the summary, the configured rule, and the assignments.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=349)** There aren't any jobs yet because this plan hasn't been executed.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=354)** So that's how you create a plan to backup resources on a schedule.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=359)** Now, let's create an on-demand backup job.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=362)** To do this, go to Protected Resources on the left navigation pane and then click "Create on-demand backup."
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=369)** First, select the resource type.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=372)** I'll set it to EBS.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=374)** Then I'll select the volume to back up.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=377)** Recently created resources may take a while to appear in the dropdown.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=383)** Next is the backup window.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=385)** We are creating an on-demand backup, so set it to create backup now then set the retention period, the vault to store it in, and provide an IAM role for permissions Then click "Create on-demand backup."
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=402)** Now, the backup job is running for the selected resource.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=406)** If you see an error message when trying to create the on-demand backup with the default IAM role, it is likely that the service role hasn't been created correctly.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=415)** To fix it, you must review the role and add any missing permissions or retry with a custom IAM role.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=422)** You will find more information about this in the documentation.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=427)** So that's about AWS backup.
>
> **[7:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=429)** As we've just seen, it simplifies backup management by centralizing it in a single service console.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/backing-up?u=76281980&t=435)** Including this in your architectural designs will help you reduce the operational overhead commonly associated with managing backups.

### 3. Automate Infrastructure

#### Why should you automate?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=0)** It's time to shift gears again.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=2)** Let's talk about automation in AWS, starting with why you should consider automation.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=8)** Let's begin by exploring the challenges with manual processes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=12)** Manual processes are prone to errors, they lack scalability, and can lead to cost inefficiencies.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=19)** With automation, you can react to changes automatically, adapting to changing demands.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=25)** For example, you can use Amazon EventBridge to monitor events of interest like the termination of an EC2 instance and react to it by sending a notification to the asset owner or launching a replacement instance.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=39)** You can automate common IT tasks like server provisioning and configuration.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=45)** AWS has services like AWS Systems Manager and AWS CloudFormation that can help achieve this requirement.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=52)** Automation also ensures consistent deployment patterns and configuration, reducing the likelihood of deployment failures.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=60)** AWS CloudFormation is a service that allows you to deploy infrastructure as code, resulting in consistent deployment each time.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=68)** With automation, you can improve your security posture by monitoring your resources or configuration and having automated remediation workflows like patching.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=78)** For example, you can use AWS Config to monitor the state or configuration of your resources and remediate non-compliant ones.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=87)** Automation optimizes your cost by deploying only the required resources to handle the workload.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=94)** For example, you can use Auto Scaling to maintain the right capacity required to handle the workload.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=100)** This means you are not under-provisioning, which can compromise performance or overprovisioning, leading to unnecessary costs.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=108)** Automation can simplify resource lifecycle management by letting the service handle tasks such as resource tagging, backups, and termination.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=119)** For example, you can use tag policies to automatically check and enforce tag compliance.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=125)** You can use AWS Backup to automate resource backups.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=130)** With automation, you can streamline and simplify complex tasks like configuring high availability and disaster recovery.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=138)** For example, you can use Elastic Load Balancing to automate high availability configuration.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=144)** You can use Amazon Route 53, a DNS management solution, to configure DNS failover for redundancy purposes.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=152)** Finally, automation can reduce time to resolution for common issues.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=157)** For example, you can use AWS Systems Manager to automate everyday tasks like installing applications or missing updates on EC2 instances and troubleshooting resource connectivity issues.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=170)** So embracing automation allows you to fully benefit from the dynamic nature and innovation in the cloud.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/why-should-you-automate?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, let's explore some of these tools.

#### CloudFormation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=0)** A common use case for automation is to deploy resources consistently and repeatedly.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=6)** AWS CloudFormation is used for this purpose.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=9)** It allows you to create a template describing all AWS resources you want to deploy.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=15)** CloudFormation then provisions and configures the resources as you described in the template.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=21)** By configuring this way, you don't need to create and configure individual resources, CloudFormation handles that.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=28)** Let's say you're creating a web application and want to test different scenarios.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=33)** The application uses different resources such as EC2 for compute, MySQL for database, an Application Load Balancer for traffic distribution, VPC for network configuration, and Route 53 for DNS configuration.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=49)** You need to deploy the application from scratch for each test scenario.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=54)** You can define the resources and their configuration in a CloudFormation template.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=59)** Simply run the template when you need to deploy the application.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=63)** Consider another use case where you'd like to deploy your solution in other regions to improve availability.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=70)** Redeploying resources and configuring them in other regions can be complex and time-consuming, especially if your solution has many components and dependencies.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=81)** With CloudFormation, you can reuse your template to create and configure resources in other regions.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=88)** A CloudFormation template is a JSON or YAML-formatted text file.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=93)** It is the blueprint for creating your resources.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=96)** Here is a template that launches an EC2 instance.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=100)** The resources section of the template describes the resource type, in this case the EC2 instance.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=106)** The property section describes properties such as the instance type, image ID, and the security group to use.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=114)** Resources created from a template are managed as a single unit called a stack.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=120)** You can update the stack to change the resources running in it.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=124)** Before making changes, you can generate a chain set which is a summary of your proposed changes.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=132)** Chain sets allow you to see how your changes might impact your running resources.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=138)** An important thing to remember is that CloudFormation can only perform actions that you have permission to do.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=145)** So to create EC2 instances using CloudFormation, you need permissions to create instances.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=152)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and deploy a stack with CloudFormation.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=158)** Once you're at the Management Console, you will find CloudFormation under the "Management and Governance" section.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=165)** Let's create a new stack.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=168)** You have three options for the template.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=171)** You can provide your own template, use a sample template, or create a template in designer.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=177)** Designer is a graphical tool with a drag and drop interface that you can use to create and modify templates.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=184)** It allows you to view resources and their relationships graphically.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=188)** To provide your own template, you can provide the S3 URL of the template, upload it as a file or sync from your git repository.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=197)** For this exercise, let's use a sample template, and here are the samples.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=203)** Let's use the simple WordPress blog template.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=207)** It will install WordPress with a local MySQL database for storage.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=213)** In the next step, give your stack a name.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=216)** I'll call it as WordPress blog.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=220)** Next, you have the parameter section.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=223)** These are parameters defined in the template and allow you to input values.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=228)** This can be useful in getting user input at runtime.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=232)** The DB name parameter is preconfigured.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=237)** I'll provide the DB password, the DB root password, and the DB username.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=244)** Next, you can provide the instance type or the instance size.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=248)** I'll set it to t2.micro.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=251)** Then you can provide the key pair name that you will use to log in to the instance, and the IP ranges from where you would like to allow SSH traffic.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=263)** In the next step, configure tags to apply to resources created by the stack.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=270)** By default, the template will apply tags for the stack ID and the stack name.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=274)** Next, you need to provide permissions to the template to deploy resources on your behalf.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=281)** By default, CloudFormation generates a temporary session from your user credentials.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=287)** So if you're logged in as a user with all the permissions that stack needs, you won't need to provide a role.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=292)** Otherwise, provide a role with the necessary permissions.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=297)** You can also use a role to limit CloudFormation's permissions.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=301)** For example, you may have administrative permissions, but you may attach a role to limit CloudFormation's access only to the Amazon S3 service.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=310)** The next section defines how to roll back in case of a failure.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=315)** When the deployment fails, you can roll back all resources or preserve successfully provisioned resources, resulting in a partial deployment.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=325)** You can use a deletion policy to delete resources or delete regardless of the attached deletion policy.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=332)** A deletion policy lets you keep the resource when the stack is deleted.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=337)** Without a deletion policy, all resources will be deleted.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=342)** The deletion policy is defined as an attribute in the template.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=346)** We'll leave these and other settings at the default and go to the next step.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=351)** Here, you can review your selections and click "Submit" to deploy the stack.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=359)** The stack is now being deployed.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/cloudformation?u=76281980&t=363)** The events tab will show you resources as they are being deployed and configured.

#### Going serverless with Lambda
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=0)** Now, let's talk about AWS Lambda.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=2)** Typically, you need servers when you want to run code for your applications.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=8)** This leads to infrastructure concerns, as you must provision and manage servers that will run the code.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=14)** AWS Lambda is a service that lets you run code without provisioning or managing servers.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=20)** You simply upload your code and Lambda will run it.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=23)** For this reason, this approach is commonly known as serverless computing.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=28)** This approach has several advantages.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=31)** You do not need to manage servers, including deploying, maintaining, and scaling them.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=36)** You save costs by paying only for the compute time you consume.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=40)** There is no charge when your code is not running.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=43)** And you can create an event-driven architecture by running code in response to events.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=48)** Lambda natively supports code written in these languages; .NET, Node.js, Go, Java, Python, and Ruby.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=59)** Because Lambda manages the underlying infrastructure that runs your code, it is abstracted from you.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=65)** This means you cannot see or access the compute instances that run your code.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=69)** You can use Lambda to process files in real-time as they're uploaded to Amazon S3.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=75)** You can build a microservices based architecture where monolithic applications are broken into smaller functions managed by Lambda.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=84)** You can build web and mobile backends by combining it with Amazon API Gateway to process API requests.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=91)** You can use Lambda to process real-time streaming data by combining with Amazon Kinesis.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=96)** You can build data pipelines for ingesting, processing, and transforming data in real time.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=102)** You can also use Lambda to execute scheduled tasks.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=106)** When combined with Amazon EventBridge, you can schedule Lambda functions to run at specific times or regular intervals.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=113)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and see how to run code in Lambda.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=119)** Once you're in the Management Console, you will find Lambda under the compute section.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=126)** Lambda organizes code as functions.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=130)** Let's create one.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=132)** You have three options to create the function; you can alter from scratch, use a blueprint, or provide a container image.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=140)** When you alter from scratch, you can select the runtime required for your code.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=145)** Let's use a blueprint.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=148)** Here, you have pre-configured code examples provided by AWS.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=152)** Let's use this blueprint that retrieves metadata for an S3 object that has been updated.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=159)** It is written in Python.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=161)** Give the function a name.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=162)** Lambda needs permissions to execute actions on your behalf.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=167)** You can use an existing role, create a new role with basic Lambda permissions, or create a new role from AWS policy templates.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=175)** Enter a name for the role.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=178)** If you've chosen to create a new role from a policy template, it will be automatically chosen for you.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=185)** Scroll down to find the function code.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=188)** The function has a handler that serves as the entry point.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=192)** It extracts event data and contacts paths to it.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=195)** The function then extracts the bucket name and the object key from the event data.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=201)** It attempts to retrieve the object from the specified bucket.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=205)** If the retrieval is successful, the function prints the object's content type.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=210)** The next section contains the trigger for the function.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=214)** In this case, it is an S3 trigger.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=216)** This option is specific to the blueprint we've chosen.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=219)** Select the desired bucket.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=222)** It must be in the same region as the Lambda function.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=225)** Then select the event types that will trigger the Lambda function.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/going-serverless-with-lambda?u=76281980&t=229)** We'll set it to put and post, which corresponds to object uploads.

#### Event-driven architecture
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=0)** An advantage of using the automation capabilities of the cloud is that you can create an event-driven architecture.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=7)** An event-driven architecture is where changes within the environment trigger code execution or initiation of workflows.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=14)** Components that make up an event-driven architecture are loosely coupled, creating a resilient and scalable infrastructure.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=21)** Amazon EventBridge is a service used to create an event-driven architecture in AWS.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=27)** It uses events to connect application components.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=31)** In simple words, it routes events from source applications to consumer applications.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=36)** For example, you can configure EventBridge to detect EC2 instance termination events.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=42)** Upon detection, EventBridge can notify an SNS topic and trigger a Lambda function to launch a replacement instance.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=50)** Or you can configure EventBridge to detect S3 object upload events.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=55)** Upon detection, EventBridge can trigger a Lambda function to process the uploaded object.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=61)** Here is how EventBridge works.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=63)** An event source generates events.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=66)** These are received by an Event bus, which is a router and delivered to destinations known as targets.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=73)** You create rules in EventBridge to evaluate events as they arrive.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=77)** Each rule checks whether an event matches the rules pattern.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=81)** If the event matches, EventBridge sends it to the target.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=84)** All right.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=85)** Now, let's go to the AWS Management Console and configure EventBridge.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=90)** Once you're in the Management Console, you will find EventBridge under the "Application Integration" section.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/aws-essential-training-for-architects-23744949/event-driven-architecture?u=76281980&t=97)** Let's create a rule to detect object uploads to an S3 bucket and invoke a Lambda function to process the object.
